<p><b>Rebecca Rose Lobo-Rushin</b> (born October 6, 1973) is an American television <a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">basketball</a> analyst and former <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_basketball" title="Women's basketball">women's basketball</a> player in the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_National_Basketball_Association" title="Women's National Basketball Association">Women's National Basketball Association</a> (WNBA) from 1997 to 2003. Lobo, at 6'4", played the <a href="/wiki/Center_(basketball)" title="Center (basketball)">center</a> position for much of her career. Lobo played <a href="/wiki/College_basketball" title="College basketball">college basketball</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Connecticut_Huskies_women%27s_basketball" title="Connecticut Huskies women's basketball">University of Connecticut</a>, where she was a member of the team that won the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Connecticut_Huskies_women%27s_basketball#Rebecca_Lobo_and_the_1995_National_Championship" title="Connecticut Huskies women's basketball">1995 national championship</a>, going 35–0 on the season in the process. In April 2017, she was announced as one of the members of the 2017 class of the <a href="/wiki/Naismith_Memorial_Basketball_Hall_of_Fame" title="Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Fame">Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Fame</a>, alongside <a href="/wiki/Tracy_McGrady" title="Tracy McGrady">Tracy McGrady</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muffet_McGraw" title="Muffet McGraw">Muffet McGraw</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_high_school_career">Early life and high school career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rebecca_Lobo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and high school career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Lobo was born in <a href="/wiki/Hartford,_Connecticut" title="Hartford, Connecticut">Hartford, Connecticut</a>, the youngest daughter of RuthAnn (née McLaughlin) and Dennis Joseph Lobo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bellingham_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bellingham-2">[2]</a></sup> Her father is of <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuban</a> descent, while her mother was of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_people" title="German people">German</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a> heritage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Lobo was raised a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scholastic_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scholastic-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThomsonGale_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-ThomsonGale-5">[5]</a></sup> Her brother Jason played basketball at <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College" title="Dartmouth College">Dartmouth College</a> and her sister Rachel played basketball at Salem State College. Lobo's mother and father were both teachers; in addition, her father coached both basketball and track and field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter285_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-porter285-6">[6]</a></sup> Raised in <a href="/wiki/Southwick,_Massachusetts" title="Southwick, Massachusetts">Southwick, Massachusetts</a>, Lobo was the state scoring record-holder with 2,740 points in her high school career for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southwick-Tolland_Regional_High_School" title="Southwick-Tolland Regional High School">Southwick-Tolland Regional High School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter285_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-porter285-6">[6]</a></sup> She held this record for 18 years until it was eclipsed by <a href="/wiki/Bilqis_Abdul-Qaadir" title="Bilqis Abdul-Qaadir">Bilqis Abdul-Qaadir</a> of the new Leadership Charter School in Springfield on January 26, 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-record_break_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-record_break-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="College">College</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rebecca_Lobo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: College">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>More than 100 colleges recruited Lobo, but she chose the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Connecticut" title="University of Connecticut">University of Connecticut</a> due to proximity and her belief in its academic excellence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter285_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-porter285-6">[6]</a></sup> She helped lead the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Connecticut_Huskies" title="Connecticut Huskies">Huskies</a> to the 1995 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NCAA_Women%27s_Division_I_Basketball_Championship" title="NCAA Women's Division I Basketball Championship">National Championship</a> with an undefeated 35-0 record. In her senior year, Lobo was the unanimous national player of the year, winning the 1995 <a href="/wiki/Naismith_College_Player_of_the_Year" title="Naismith College Player of the Year">Naismith College Player of the Year</a> award, the <a href="/wiki/Wade_Trophy" title="Wade Trophy">Wade Trophy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press_Women%27s_College_Basketball_Player_of_the_Year" title="Associated Press Women's College Basketball Player of the Year">AP Player of the Year</a> award, the <a href="/wiki/USBWA_Women%27s_National_Player_of_the_Year" title="USBWA Women's National Player of the Year">USBWA Player of the Year</a> award, the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Sports_Award_(basketball)" title="Honda Sports Award (basketball)">Honda Sports Award</a> for basketball, and the <a href="/wiki/WBCA_Player_of_the_Year" title="WBCA Player of the Year">WBCA Player of the Year</a> award. Lobo was awarded the prestigious <a href="/wiki/Honda-Broderick_Cup" title="Honda-Broderick Cup">Honda-Broderick Cup</a> for 1994-95, presented to the athlete "most deserving of recognition as the Collegiate Woman Athlete of the Year".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-8">[8]</a></sup> She was a member of the inaugural class of inductees to the University of Connecticut women's basketball "Huskies of Honor" recognition program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huskies_of_Honor_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huskies_of_Honor-9">[9]</a></sup> Lobo was named the 1995 Sportswoman of the Year (in the team category) by the Women's Sports Foundation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSF_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSF-10">[10]</a></sup> Lobo was the first player in the Big East Conference ever to earn first team all American honors for both basketball and academics.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="USA_Basketball">USA Basketball</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rebecca_Lobo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: USA Basketball">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Lobo was named to the USA U18 team (then called the Junior World Championship Qualifying Team) in 1992. The team competed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guanajuato,_Guanajuato" title="Guanajuato, Guanajuato">Guanajuato, Mexico</a> in August 1992. The team won their first four games, then lost 80–70 to Brazil, finishing with the silver medal for the event, but qualifying for the 1993 world games. Lobo averaged 6.8 points per game during the event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1992_U18_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-1992_U18-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Lobo continued with the team to the 1993 U19 World Championship (then called the Junior World Championship). The team won five games and lost two, but that left them in seventh place. Lobo averaged 7.7 points per game and recorded six blocks, highest on the team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1993_U19_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-1993_U19-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional">Professional</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rebecca_Lobo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Professional">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1995 Lobo passed through tryouts to join the national team, which later became the US team for the 1996 Olympics in Atlanta, GA. Though her minutes on the floor were few, Lobo shared in the gold medal. In 1997, the WNBA was formed and enjoyed its inaugural season, and Lobo was assigned to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Liberty" title="New York Liberty">New York Liberty</a> during the league's first player allocations on January 22, 1997.  The first season the Liberty fell to the Houston Comets in the WNBA Finals. Lobo suffered a setback in 1999, tearing her left <a href="/wiki/Anterior_cruciate_ligament" title="Anterior cruciate ligament">anterior cruciate ligament</a> and her meniscus in the first game of the season. In 1999, she was selected to the inaugural WNBA All Star team but could not play because of the injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_ACL_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_ACL-13">[13]</a></sup>  In 2002, she was traded to the <a href="/wiki/Houston_Comets" title="Houston Comets">Houston Comets</a> in exchange for Houston's second-round selection (26th overall) in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2002_WNBA_Draft" title="2002 WNBA Draft">2002 WNBA Draft</a>.  The next season she was traded to the <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Sun" title="Connecticut Sun">Connecticut Sun</a>, where she retired in 2003. Lobo also played two seasons in the National Women's Basketball league with the Springfield Spirit 2002 through 2003.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports_announcing">Sports announcing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rebecca_Lobo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Sports announcing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Today, Lobo is a reporter and color analyst for <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a> with a focus on women's college basketball and WNBA games.
</p>
<h3><span id="Women.27s_Basketball_Hall_of_Fame"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Women's_Basketball_Hall_of_Fame">Women's Basketball Hall of Fame</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rebecca_Lobo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Women's Basketball Hall of Fame">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lobo was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Basketball_Hall_of_Fame" title="Women's Basketball Hall of Fame">Women's Basketball Hall of Fame</a> as part of the class of 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lobo_WBHOF_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lobo_WBHOF-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WBHOF_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-WBHOF-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the induction ceremony, Lobo was introduced by her college coach, <a href="/wiki/Geno_Auriemma" title="Geno Auriemma">Geno Auriemma</a> who said, in part:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>No one in all the years that I've been there, has had the impact on the court and off the court, that Rebecca has had and has continued both in the WNBA, as being one of the founders, both as a representative of our university, as a member of the board of trustees, continuing to promote the game on ESPN, and all the other things that Rebecca has done to further the role model that she is, for all the young people that looked up to her, emulated what she has always been, a great student, a great athlete, a great person, someone that I've cherished to have had the opportunity to work with, and to call my friend, and now to call my boss.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Geno_Auriemma" title="Geno Auriemma">Geno Auriemma</a>, <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lobo_Induction_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lobo_Induction-16">[16]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Lobo talked about touring the museum and seeing the exhibit of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All_American_Red_Heads" title="All American Red Heads">All American Red Heads</a> team. She talked about the influence of her grandmothers on her life, her parents, and others who helped her with her career.  Then she related an anecdote about her daughter, epitomizing how things have changed for women in the sports world:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>But two years ago, a year and a half ago, my oldest daughter, who was 4 ½, and my husband was watching UConn men, playing on the television in the living room, and my daughter walked in the room and looked at the TV and said to Steve, "Are those boys playing?"
</p><p>And I said, "yes".
</p><p>
And my daughter said, "I didn't know boys played basketball".</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Lobo, <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lobo_Induction_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lobo_Induction-16">[16]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_and_personal_life">Family and personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rebecca_Lobo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Family and personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On April 12, 2003, Rebecca changed her last name from Lobo to Lobo-Rushin after marrying <i><a href="/wiki/Sports_Illustrated" title="Sports Illustrated">Sports Illustrated</a></i>  writer <a href="/wiki/Steve_Rushin" title="Steve Rushin">Steve Rushin</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basketball_Hall_of_Fame" title="Basketball Hall of Fame">Basketball Hall of Fame</a> in <a href="/wiki/Springfield,_Massachusetts" title="Springfield, Massachusetts">Springfield, Massachusetts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marriage_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-marriage-17">[17]</a></sup>  They have four children (three daughters and one son). In Steve Rushin's words: "The first words Rebecca Lobo ever spoke to me when we met in a Manhattan bar in 2001 were: 'Aren't you the guy who just mocked women's basketball in <i>Sports Illustrated</i>?' I blushed, broke out in a flop sweat and said, 'Yes.'  A few weeks earlier, I had written that although Wilt Chamberlain claimed to have slept with 20,000 women in his lifetime, I had once slept with 7,138 women in a single night: We were all snoring in the stands at a WNBA game. It was an appalling line, and I was eager to change the subject, but Lobo wouldn't let it go. 'We get 15,000 fans at <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">Madison Square Garden</a>,' she said. 'How many women's games have you ever been to, anyway?' Hanging my head in shame, I admitted that I had never attended a women's basketball game before ridiculing it in a national magazine. So she invited me to one of her New York Liberty games, I accepted, and 23 months later we were married."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartford_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hartford-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breast_cancer_advocate_and_health_spokesperson">Breast cancer advocate and health spokesperson</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rebecca_Lobo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Breast cancer advocate and health spokesperson">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1996, Lobo and her late mother, Ruth Ann Lobo, collaborated on a book entitled <i>The Home Team</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amazon_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-amazon-19">[19]</a></sup> which dealt with Ruth Ann's battle with <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">breast cancer</a>.  They also founded the RuthAnn and Rebecca Lobo Scholarship, which offers a scholarship to the UConn School of Allied Health for Hispanic students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dailynews_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-dailynews-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rebecca was the 1996 spokesperson for the <a href="/wiki/Lee_National_Denim_Day" title="Lee National Denim Day">Lee National Denim Day</a> fund raiser which raises millions of dollars for <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">breast cancer</a> research and education.
</p><p>Starting in 2000, Lobo served as national spokesperson and backer for Body1.com, a consumer-targeted network of sites providing interactive content-rich information on medical technologies that treat ailments and diseases specific to body parts. Due to her recurring problems with a torn <a href="/wiki/Anterior_cruciate_ligament" title="Anterior cruciate ligament">anterior cruciate ligament</a>, (ACL), she campaigned to raise awareness of knee injury risks in women. Lobo shared her story with others suffering from the same type of injury and strongly advocated for patient self-education via the Internet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acl_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-acl-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_honors">Awards and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rebecca_Lobo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Awards and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rebecca_Lobo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
